\begin{document}

  \maketitle
  \thispagestyle{empty}
  
  \begin{abstract} 
  \noindent We examine the price elasticity of demand for the aggregate stock market in a general equilibrium framework that incorporates rich investor heterogeneity, passive demand, and financial constraints. Using global perturbation techniques, we analytically characterize market elasticity and find that it critically depends on investors' cross-price elasticity---that is, the sensitivity of demand for risky assets to changes in the interest rate. When cross-elasticity is nonzero, the market remains infinitely elastic if passive investors hold the efficient share of risky assets, regardless of how price-inelastic individual investors are. In contrast, portfolio inflows have a positive price impact when risk is misallocated in the economy.
  % We study the price elasticity of demand of the aggregate stock market %the aggregate market elasticity %inelastic market hypothesis of \cite{gabaix2020search} 
  % in a general equilibrium model with rich investor heterogeneity, passive demand, and financial constraints. 
  % We use global perturbation techniques to characterize the market elasticity analytically. 
  % We find that the aggregate market elasticity depends crucially on investors' cross-price elasticity, i.e., the sensitivity of the demand for risky assets with respect to the interest rate. 
  % When the cross-elasticity is non-zero, the market is infinitely elastic if passive investors hold the efficient amount of risky assets, regardless of how price-inelastic individual investors are. 
  % In contrast, portfolio inflows have a positive price impact when risk is misallocated in the economy. 
  %  % We estimate our quantitative model and show that the model generates inelastic markets and high return volatility.
   
  
  % We study the origins of the inelastic market hypothesis of \cite{gabaix2020search} by presenting a general equilibrium model with rich investor heterogeneity, passive demand, and financial constraints. We show how different frictions can make the aggregate market more inelastic, which is necessary to amplify the price impact of portfolio flows. We use global perturbation techniques to characterize the aggregate market elasticity analytically. We estimate our quantitative model and provide a structural decomposition of the market elasticity. We document which frictions are quantitatively important to generate inelastic markets and, consequently, high market volatility. 
  
%  We study the role of portfolio flows in determining asset prices and market volatility in the context of a quantitative general equilibrium model with rich heterogeneity and portfolio frictions. 
%  We present a new analytical characterization of the aggregate market elasticity. We show how different frictions can make the aggregate market more inelastic, which are necessary to amplify the effect of portfolio flows.
%  We also estimate our quantitative model and provide a structural decomposition of the market elasticity. We document which frictions %considered by the macro-finance literature 
%  are quantitatively important to generate inelastic markets and, consequently, high market volatility. Finally, we propose an algorithm to compute the equilibrium of heterogeneous-agents structural models similar to ours. By representing the value functions as infinitely wide neural networks (neural tangent kernel), the problem of minimizing the Bellman equation residuals is shown to be convex, and therefore globally convergent. We leverage this solution method to estimate our model faster than traditional approaches by orders of magnitude.
  \vspace{10mm} 
  \bigskip
  
  \noindent 
  \textsc{\textbf{Keywords}}: Aggregate market elasticity, risk missallocation, demand shocks, demand shifters, excess volatility, asset pricing. 
  
  \medskip
  \noindent
  \textsc{\textbf{JEL Classification}}: G12, G11, G21.   
\end{abstract}

\section{Introduction}
% \textcolor{red}{\textbf{TO DO:}
% \begin{itemize}
%     \item replace ``flows'' with ``demand shifters'' throughout the paper
%     \item add the motivation plot from the St. Louis Fed conference
%     \item rewrite the intro accordingly
% \end{itemize}}
%Why are asset prices so volatile? 
\noindent Why is the stock market so volatile? This question has long been the subject of extensive research and debate.
% More recently, \citet{giglio2018excess} demonstrate that the prices of long-maturity instruments exhibit significantly greater variability than predicted by standard asset pricing models.} 
Existing explanations often invoke the so-called ``dark matter of asset pricing,'' where unobserved factors account for the majority of price fluctuations.\footnote{For work on excess volatility tests, see, e.g., \cite{leroy1981present}, \cite{shiller1981,shiller1992market}, and \cite{cochrane1992explaining}. See \citet{chen2024measuring} for the discussion of ``dark matter" in asset pricing.} 
More recent demand-based evidence highlights the role of asset price movements in response to portfolio flows as a potential explanation for the origins of the excess market volatility.
% \footnote{For early evidence on the role of portfolio flows and asset prices, see \citet{Deuskar/Johnson/2011}.} 
Given the relatively small magnitude of these flows in the data, as shown in the left panel of Figure~\ref{fig:facts-vol-flow}, this hypothesis requires markets to be highly \textit{inelastic} on average, meaning that even small changes in quantities lead to large price responses \citep{gabaix2020search}. 
Moreover, as illustrated in the right panel of Figure~\ref{fig:facts-vol-flow}, the volatility multiplier---defined as the ratio of return volatility to flows---is time-varying and countercyclical, spiking during crisis periods.

This new evidence naturally raises several important questions: Why is the aggregate stock market so inelastic? Can standard frictionless asset pricing models generate such low levels of market elasticity? If not, what frictions are necessary to quantitatively explain the impact of portfolio flows on asset prices and match the observed dynamics of the volatility multiplier? Addressing these questions is key to understanding and disentangling the forces driving inelastic markets and, consequently, high market volatility.
% This new evidence naturally leads to several new questions: Why is the aggregate stock market so inelastic? Can the standard frictionless asset pricing models generate low levels of market elasticity? 
% If not, what are the frictions necessary to quantitatively explain the impact of portfolio flows on asset prices and match the dynamics of the volatility multiplier? 
% Addressing these questions helps us understand and disentangle different forces behind inelastic markets and, consequently, high market volatility. 
%Given the relatively small magnitude of these flows in the data, this hypothesis requires markets to be very \textit{inelastic}, such that small movements in quantities induce a large price response. This new explanation naturally leads to several new questions: Why are markets so inelastic? Can the standard frictionless models generate low levels of market elasticity? If not, what are the frictions necessary to quantitatively explain the impact of portfolio flows on asset prices? Only by addressing these questions can we disentangle the different forces that lead to inelastic markets and, consequently, high market volatility. 

%\todo[inline]{Discuss the fact that the market elasticity captures the flow between two markets. In particular, how the simultaneous response of interest rate and risk premium are crucial in shaping the macro elasticity. This is in contrast to the micro elasticity, which focus in flows from one stock into another, where the response of the money market is less relevant. However, characterizing these general equilibrium effects is often challenging. This explains why GK abstract from the interest rate effects. We need a framework to study the importance of these GE effects. }


This paper develops a general equilibrium model with investor heterogeneity and portfolio frictions to address the questions outlined above. The general equilibrium (GE) perspective is essential, as the key object for studying the price impact of flows is the \textit{macro (aggregate) elasticity}---the change in the aggregate stock market value in response to a \$1 flow from bonds into stocks.\footnote{Throughout the paper, we use the terms ``aggregate elasticity'' and ``macro elasticity'' interchangeably.}
When deriving the expression for aggregate elasticity, we account for market-wide adjustments in investors’ portfolio holdings across stocks and risk-free bonds, emphasizing the simultaneous response of both the interest rate and the risk premium to flows.\footnote{This contrasts with micro elasticity, which focuses on flows between individual stocks, where changes in the money market play a smaller role.} Understanding these general equilibrium effects---particularly the impact of flows on the risk-free rate and risk premium---is crucial for characterizing the macro elasticity.
% This paper presents a general equilibrium model with rich investor heterogeneity and portfolio frictions to answer the questions posed above.
% % \footnote{Our work closely relates to \citeauthor{johnson2006dynamic} (\citeyear{johnson2006dynamic}, \citeyear{johnson2008volume}, and \citeyear{johnson2009liquid}), who provide an early characterization of market liquidity in frictionless environments.}
% The general equilibrium (GE) perspective is crucial, as the relevant object to study the price impact of flows is the \textit{macro (aggregate) elasticity}:  %which takes into account the market-wide implications of change in investors' portfolio holdings. We are interested in 
% the change in the aggregate stock market value in response to a \$1 flow into stocks from bonds.\footnote{Throughout the paper, we use the terms ``aggregate elasticity'' and ``macro elasticity'' interchangeably.} When deriving the expression for this aggregate elasticity, we take into account the market-wide implications of the change in investors' portfolio holdings across stocks and risk-free bonds, emphasizing the simultaneous response of interest rate and risk premium to flows.\footnote{This is in contrast with micro elasticity, which focuses on flows from one stock into another, where the response of the money market is less relevant.} Considering the GE effects of the impact of flows on risk-free rate and risk premium are thus crucial for understanding macro elasticity. Importantly, the behavioral element in the general equilibrium model in \cite{gabaix2020search} leads to a constant interest rate, missing the risk-free rate impact on macro elasticity discussed above.\footnote{\cite{johnson2006dynamic} studies the equilibrium price change in response to a perturbation in the risky asset supply. His definition of liquidity, although different from ours, also allows for the interest rate to vary when stock prices change.}

\begin{figure}[!t]
    \centering
    \subfloat{\resizebox{0.49\textwidth}{!}{\input{figures/time-series-vol-flow.tex}}}
    \subfloat{\resizebox{0.49\textwidth}{!}{\input{figures/time-series-vol-multiplier.tex}}}
    \caption{Quarterly flows and return volatility.\\ The left panel plots quarterly flows (in blue) and return volatility (in red). The right panel plots the ``volatility multiplier,'' defined as the ratio of return volatility to flows. Source: Flow of Funds and CRSP.}\label{fig:facts-vol-flow}
\end{figure}

Allowing the interest rate to endogenously respond to portfolio-flow shocks plays a crucial role in determining the aggregate market elasticity.\footnote{A portfolio-flow shock corresponds to an asymmetric shock to the demand of risky assets, affecting only a subset of investors and leading to a portfolio reallocation in equilibrium.} If investors' cross-price elasticity is zero---meaning that demand for the risky asset is independent of the interest rate---then aggregate market elasticity is simply an average of individual investors' price elasticities, consistent with \citet{gabaix2020search}. In this case, the market remains inelastic as long as investor demand is relatively unresponsive to price changes. 
Importantly, the behavioral element of their general equilibrium model leads to a constant interest rate.
% , thereby missing the role of the risk-free rate in shaping macro elasticity.
In contrast, we show that if cross-elasticity is nonzero, and interest rates are allowed to move, the market can become infinitely elastic, regardless of how price-inelastic individual investors are.\footnote{\cite{johnson2006dynamic} studies equilibrium price changes in response to shifts in risky asset supply. While his definition of liquidity differs from ours, it also allows for interest rate adjustments when stock prices change.}

The disconnect between individual and market elasticities arises from a general equilibrium effect. 
In partial equilibrium, when passive investors shift funds from the risky asset to the riskless asset, the price of the risky asset tends to decline, increasing the risk premium and incentivizing active investors to raise their exposure. However, in general equilibrium, the interest rate must adjust to clear the bond market in response to the higher demand for the riskless asset. The decline in interest rates counteracts the effect of the risk premium on the price of the risky asset. We show that if passive investors initially hold an efficient share of the risky asset, the drop in interest rates fully counteracts the change in the risk premium, resulting in no impact on the price of the risky asset following a portfolio flow shock.

This result highlights that individual investors being price-inelastic, as the evidence suggests, is not sufficient to generate inelastic markets. This raises the question of what drives low aggregate market elasticity in general equilibrium. We find that the initial allocation of risk in the economy plays a crucial role. A shock to the portfolio of passive investors effectively redistributes risk between active and passive investors. If risk is initially allocated efficiently, small portfolio adjustments have no impact on aggregate savings behavior, which ultimately determines the price-dividend ratio. In equilibrium, the interest rate fully offsets the change in the risk premium. However, if risk is initially misallocated, portfolio reallocation directly impacts aggregate savings behavior, preventing the interest rate from fully offsetting changes in the risk premium. Through the lens of the model, inelastic markets serve as an indicator of inefficiencies in risk allocation.
 
We first derive these results within a simple two-period model and then demonstrate that the same intuition extends to a fully dynamic framework. Unlike much of the existing macro-finance literature, we go beyond a two-agent setting and incorporate rich investor heterogeneity. By considering a broader set of investors, we capture the effects of asset reallocations across different sectors, such as mutual funds, households, broker-dealers, and foreign investors.

Several factors can contribute to market inelasticity, including passive demand, institutional investment mandates, and limits to arbitrage.\footnote{See \cite{gabaix2020search} for more details.} We introduce two key frictions: passive investment and margin constraints. Households are assumed to follow a passive investment strategy rather than actively trading in financial markets, consistent with empirical evidence (e.g., \citealp{brunnermeier2008wealth}). The economy also includes active investors who face margin constraints, which are widely recognized in the literature as crucial for market outcomes and the behavior of financial intermediaries.\footnote{See, e.g., \cite{brunnermeier2009market}, \cite{garleanu2011margin}, \cite{chabakauri2013dynamic}, and \cite{adrian2014procyclical}.}

We show that, in addition to the wealth distribution among agents, market volatility is influenced by both passive flow shocks and aggregate market elasticity. To explain excess market volatility, both factors are necessary. In highly inelastic markets, flow shocks are required to generate additional volatility; without them, inelasticity alone does not increase volatility. Likewise, if markets are infinitely elastic, even large flow shocks do not create excess volatility.
% %We show how the market volatility depends on the aggregate elasticity and flow shocks.  To explain excess volatility, we both need inelastic market and the flow shocks to be present. 
% We show that in addition to the wealth distribution among agents, the market volatility also depends on the flow shocks and the aggregate market elasticity. To explain the excess market volatility, both flow shocks and inelastic markets are necessary. There is no additional impact on market volatility with highly inelastic markets in the absence of flow shocks. Similarly, there is no excess volatility with high flow shocks and infinitely elastic markets.  

Studying market elasticity in economies with frictions is challenging, as closed-form solutions are typically unavailable. To address this, we apply global perturbation techniques to derive closed-form expressions for market elasticity. Our analysis reveals that aggregate elasticity is both state-dependent and time-varying, influenced not only by the wealth distribution between active and passive investors but also by how wealth is allocated among active agents. In the absence of frictions, the market is infinitely elastic. However, the introduction of passive investors increases the price impact of flows relative to frictionless markets. Preference heterogeneity makes the aggregate market more elastic due to risk misallocation, as more risk-averse passive investors require a higher risk premium to absorb additional risky asset supply. Finally, binding leverage constraints amplify the price impact of flows. Our findings highlight the crucial role of general equilibrium effects and investor heterogeneity in driving market inelasticity.
% We first obtain an analytical characterization of the market elasticity. 
% Studying the market elasticity in economies with frictions is challenging, as closed-form solutions are typically unavailable. 
% We apply global perturbation techniques that enable us to obtain closed-form expressions for the market elasticity. 
% We show that the aggregate elasticity is state-dependent and time-varying: it depends not only on the wealth distribution among active and passive investors but also on how wealth is distributed within active agents. 
% We show that the market is infinitely elastic in the absence of frictions. 
% Introducing passive investors leads to a larger price impact relative to frictionless markets. Preference heterogeneity makes the aggregate market more elastic due to risk misallocation: more risk-averse passive investors demand a higher risk premium to hold the additional supply of the risky asset due to flows. Finally, binding leverage constraints amplify the price impact of flows. We emphasize the critical role GE effects and investor heterogeneity play in generating inelastic markets. 
% \todo[inline]{Explain how our results differ from \cite{gabaix2020search}}

We then decompose the aggregate elasticity into components driven by the impact of passive flows on the risk premium, the risk-free rate, and the drift of the risky asset price. This decomposition highlights the general equilibrium effects of passive flows, as the risk-free rate and risk premium influence aggregate elasticity in opposing directions.

Lastly, we conduct a quantitative assessment of the model, which involves solving and estimating a high-dimensional asset pricing framework. A key contribution of our work is demonstrating how to computationally manage this complexity. We then evaluate the role of various frictions in explaining excess market volatility.

% We then decompose the aggregate elasticity into components due to the impact of passive flows on the risk premium, risk-free rate, and the drift of the risky asset price. This decomposition reemphasizes the GE impact of passive flows. The risk-free rate and risk premium move the aggregate elasticity in opposite directions. 


% Lastly, we provide a quantitative assessment of the model, which requires solving and estimating a high-dimensional asset pricing model. Part of our contribution is demonstrating how we can handle this problem computationally. We quantitatively assess the role different frictions play in explaining the excess market volatility. %Since the distribution of wealth across different agents is a state variable in our model setup, standard approaches based on solving partial differential equations (PDEs), commonly used in the literature in problems with low-dimensional heterogeneity, do not apply to our setting. Models featuring only two types of agents can be solved by appealing to the homotheticity condition and eliminating agents' wealth from state variables in the system of PDEs. This reduces the problem to a system of ordinary differential equations (ODEs) that can be easily solved with standard solvers. With multiple agents, this approach naturally fails.

%The model we consider allows for different channels considered by the macro-finance literature. We assess which one of these channels is quantitatively important to explain the degree of market volatility. Since the distribution of wealth across different agents is a state variable in our model setup, standard approaches based on solving partial differential equations (PDEs), commonly used in the literature in problems with low-dimensional heterogeneity, do not apply to our setting. Models featuring only two types of agents can be solved by appealing to the homotheticity condition and eliminating agents' wealth from state variables in the system of PDEs. This reduces the problem to a system of ordinary differential equations (ODEs) that can be easily solved with standard solvers. With multiple agents, this approach naturally fails.

%Our approach, on the other hand, relies on solving the perturbed equilibrium equations. Our perturbation technique is done in the space of parameters, and not around a point in the state space, and therefore the solution is global in the states. 
%Additionally, we show that if the basis functions used to represent the value functions is linear in a set of coefficients, the problem of finding the coefficients that minimize the mean squared errors of the HJB residuals is convex. Finally, we approximate the value functions using the recently developed neural tangent kernel (\citealp{neural_tangent}), that shows that infinitely-wide neural networks can be represented with a suitable linear basis.
%
%In general equilibrium, in addition to the value functions, we need to determine endogenous objects like the risk-free rate, prices of risk, and the dividend yield of the risky asset. In our model setup, all of these objects can be written as closed-form expressions of the value functions (and their drifts and diffusions), so the problem reduces to a problem of computing the value functions of the different types of agents. 
%% As a proof of concept, we have implemented a simplified version of the model with up to 10 different types of agents. 
%This approach also facilitates calibration/estimation. Indeed, by including key model parameters as inputs for the neural networks, we can approximate value functions (and other equilibrium objects) for entire ranges of parameters. Having solved for the equilibrium objects for a continuum of parameters makes it easier to conduct simulation-based estimation like the Simulated Method of Moments or Indirect Inference.
\subsubsection*{Related literature}
% \textcolor{red}{Need to add more here. See GK's citations.}
Our work relates to the literature on macro demand elasticity that studies the impact of asset flows on the aggregate stock market price. The papers closest to ours are \cite{johnson2006dynamic} and \cite{gabaix2020search}. In a representative agent model, \cite{johnson2006dynamic} perturbs the risky asset supply and finds finite elasticity even in the frictionless Lucas economy. \cite{gabaix2020search} introduce a behavioral element that fixes interest rates and generates large price impacts in a setting with representative households investing in constrained funds. We build on this literature by developing a general equilibrium model with heterogeneous investors and financial frictions, showing that both GE effects and constraints are central for obtaining inelastic markets.\footnote{For more on macro elasticity, see \cite{johnson2008volume,johnson2009liquid}, \cite{Deuskar/Johnson/2011}, \cite{Li/Pearson/Zhang/2020}, \cite{Hartzmark/Solomon/2021}, among others.} 

The macro elasticity literature contrasts with the larger body of work on micro elasticity, which examines relative price changes across individual stocks (e.g., \citealp{Shleifer/1986}; \citealp{Harris/Gurel/1986}; \citealp{chang2015regression}; \citealp{pavlova2021benchmarking}; \citealp{Schmickler/2020}). Evidence suggests that micro elasticities are much larger than aggregate elasticities, consistent with the fact that different stocks are closer substitutes for each other than are equities and bonds. 

A further strand of work is demand-system asset pricing pioneered by \cite{Koijen/Yogo/19}. Their framework estimates asset-level demand slopes from flows and prices. We extend this approach by incorporating cross-elasticities across asset classes, following \cite{fuchs2023demand} and \cite{haddad2025causal}. Even small substitution between equities and bonds can produce large effects on macro elasticities, since portfolio rebalancing amplifies the aggregate price response to flows. This highlights that aggregate inelasticity depends not only on own-asset demand but also on cross-asset substitution. 

In addition, our framework extends the analysis to dynamic settings. \cite{Binsbergen-David-Opp2025} show that static IO-style estimators can be biased when shocks are persistent, while \cite{he-kondor-Li2025demand} emphasize that supply shocks affect both expected returns and the endogenous risk structure. Building on these insights, we show that persistence and the evolution of premia are crucial for aggregate elasticity, shaping both short-run substitution and long-run adjustments in volatility and covariance. 

We also connect to the literature on intermittent rebalancing, most notably \cite{ChienColeLustig2012}. Their model shows how infrequent portfolio adjustments slow demand responses and create persistent deviations from frictionless benchmarks. In our setting, passive investors play a similar role: incomplete rebalancing prolongs adjustment to shocks, reinforcing the persistence of price impacts. This mechanism complements the dynamic considerations above by showing that sluggish rebalancing is itself an important source of aggregate inelasticity. 

Finally, our paper relates to preferred-habitat models such as \cite{VayanosVila2021}, along with recent contributions by \cite{KekreLenelMainardi2025} and \cite{RayDroste2025}. These studies emphasize intermediary frictions and market segmentation as sources of inelasticity. By contrast, our framework highlights wealth effects: shifts in aggregate wealth directly alter portfolio demand and thereby macro elasticities. This mechanism, absent in the preferred-habitat literature, shows that even without intermediary frictions, wealth effects alone can generate powerful amplification of asset price movements. 

% Our work relates to the literature on macro demand elasticity that studies the impact of asset flows on the aggregate stock market price. The papers closest to ours are \cite{johnson2006dynamic} and \cite{gabaix2020search}. In a representative agent model, \cite{johnson2006dynamic} conducts a different exercise than ours by perturbing the risky asset supply and finds finite elasticity even in the frictionless Lucas economy. \cite{gabaix2020search} present a model with a behavioral element that leads to constant interest rates to achieve a large price impact in a setting with representative households who can invest in funds subject to mandates. We present a general equilibrium model with rich investor heterogeneity and financial constraints and show that both GE effects and frictions are essential for obtaining inelastic markets.\footnote{For more on macro elasticity, see \cite{johnson2008volume,johnson2009liquid}, \cite{Deuskar/Johnson/2011}, \cite{Li/Pearson/Zhang/2020}, \cite{Hartzmark/Solomon/2021}, and others.} 

% The macro elasticity literature contrasts with the much larger one on micro elasticity, which examines the change in the relative price of two stocks if one buys \$1 of one and sells \$1 of the other (e.g., \citealp{Shleifer/1986}; \citealp{Harris/Gurel/1986}; \citealp{chang2015regression}; \citealp{pavlova2021benchmarking}; \citealp{Schmickler/2020}). The evidence in the literature suggests that the micro elasticity is much larger than the aggregate elasticity, which is the object of interest in our paper, given that different stocks are closer substitutes than the stock and bond market indices. 

% % Our paper is also related to the theoretical microstructure literature (e.g., \citealp{Kyle/1985}). Unlike this literature, in our setting asset flows affect the macro elasticity through their impact on the risk premium and the risk-free rate. \cite{Campbell/Kyle/1993} look at the interaction of noise traders and smart money, analogous to our discussion of the interaction between passive and active investors. However, they consider a model with CARA preferences and constant interest rates, thus abstracting from the role of wealth distribution and the GE effects we emphasize.

% A further strand of related work is demand system asset pricing pioneered by \cite{Koijen/Yogo/19}. Their framework introduces an empirical demand system for financial assets, where asset-level flows and prices are linked through estimated elasticities. We generalize this approach by incorporating cross-elasticities across asset classes, following the recent insights of \cite{fuchs2023demand} and \cite{haddad2025causal}. These studies demonstrate that ignoring substitution patterns across assets can bias estimated demand slopes. In our setting, even small cross-elasticities between equities and bonds generate large effects on macro elasticities, since portfolio rebalancing amplifies the aggregate price response to flows. This highlights that measured market inelasticity reflects not only own-asset demand slopes but also the broader substitution structure across assets. 

% In addition, our framework extends the analysis to dynamic settings. \cite{Binsbergen-David-Opp2025} show that static IO-style demand estimators can be severely biased when price shocks are persistent, while \cite{he-kondor-Li2025demand} emphasize that residual supply shocks alter both expected returns and the endogenous risk structure of assets. Building on these insights, we demonstrate that dynamics are crucial for understanding aggregate elasticity: the persistence of shocks and the evolution of risk premia shape both short-run substitution effects and long-run adjustments in volatility and covariance. 

% Our analysis also connects to the literature on intermittent rebalancing, most notably \cite{ChienColeLustig2012}. Their model highlights how infrequent portfolio adjustments by households slow the adjustment of demand and create persistent deviations from frictionless benchmarks. In our setting, passive investors generate a similar force: because they do not fully rebalance, demand adjusts only gradually to shocks, amplifying the persistence of price impacts. This mechanism complements the dynamic considerations above by showing that sluggish rebalancing is itself an important source of aggregate inelasticity. 

% Finally, our framework relates to the class of preferred-habitat models, including \cite{VayanosVila2021}, and recent contributions by \cite{KekreLenelMainardi2025} and \cite{RayDroste2025}. These papers emphasize intermediary frictions and market segmentation as drivers of inelasticity. By contrast, our model introduces wealth effects as a novel channel: shifts in aggregate wealth directly alter portfolio demand and therefore macro elasticities. This channel is absent in \cite{VayanosVila2021} and subsequent work, where investor risk tolerance or intermediary capacity are the primary sources of inelasticity. We show that even without intermediary frictions, wealth effects alone can generate powerful amplification of price impacts at the macro level.



\section{A Simple Model of the Aggregate Market Elasticity}\label{sec:simple}

In this section, we study the determination of the aggregate market elasticity in a simple general equilibrium demand-based asset pricing model. 
To keep the discussion as simple as possible, we directly specify investors' demands, without deriving them from utility functions. 
In Section \ref{sec:model}, we provide a micro-founded version of investors' demands in a dynamic heterogeneous-agents model.

\subsection{A \texorpdfstring{$\boldsymbol{2\times 2 \times 2}$}{Lg} asset pricing model }

Consider a two-period economy with two assets, a risky asset and a riskless asset, and two agents, a passive investor $(p)$ and an active investor $(a)$. 
A fraction $\omega_j$ of investors is of type $j \in \{a,p\}$.
The risky asset pays a random dividend $Y'$ in the second period. 
The price of the risky asset is denoted by  $P$, and the price of the riskless asset is denoted by $R_f^{-1}$.
% The portfolio share of passive investors is exogenously given, while the portfolio share of active investors is endogenous and responds to movements in the risk premium.

Investors' budget constraints in the two periods are given by $ P Q_j + R_f^{-1} B_j + C_j = W_j$ and 
$C_j' = Y' Q_j + B_j$, 
% \begin{equation}
%     P Q_j + R_f^{-1} B_j + C_j = W_j, \qquad C_j' = Y' Q_j + B_j,\nonumber
% \end{equation}
where $Q_j$ denotes the number of shares of the risky asset held by investor $j$, $B_j$ is the number of riskless bonds, $C_j$ denotes initial consumption, and $C_j'$ denotes consumption in the second period. 
Initial wealth is given by $W_j = (P+Y) Q_{j,-1} > 0$.

Let $\alpha_j \equiv \frac{P Q_j}{W_j - C_j}$ denote the portfolio share of the risky asset for investor $j$. 
For the passive investor, the portfolio share is exogeneously given, that is, $\alpha_p = \overline{\alpha}_p$, where $\overline{\alpha}_p \geq 0$ is a fixed parameter.
For the active investor, the portfolio share is a function of the risk premium: $    \alpha_a = g_{a}(\pi)$, where $\pi \equiv \log \frac{1}{R_f}\mathbb{E}[\frac{Y'}{P}]$ represents the log risk premium and $g'_a(\cdot) \geq 0$.
% \footnote{The portfolio share of the active investor is allowed to depend on dividend $Y$ and the distribution of $Y'$. Since we keep the distribution of cash flows fixed throughout the analysis in this two-period model, we do not explicitly include them in the notation.}

Investor $j$'s consumption is given by $ C_j = c_{j}( r , \pi) W_j$, where $r \equiv \log R$ is the log risk-free rate. The consumption-wealth ratio, $c_j$, is a function of both the interest rate and risk premium. 
% Moreover, this specification imposes that the interest rate and risk premium affects the consumption-wealth ratio in the same way, i.e. $\frac{\partial c_j(r,\pi)}{\partial r} = \frac{\partial c_j(r,\pi)}{\partial \pi}$.
% We derive this property as result in the context of our micro-founded model in Section \ref{sec:elasticity}.
If $c_{j}(r, \pi)$ decreased (increased) with $r$, then the investor saves more (less) when the interest rate is high, reflecting a standard substitution (income) effect.
% Therefore, $\frac{\partial c_j}{\partial r}< 0$ is analogous to the case where the substitution effect dominates the income effect in standard consumption-savings problems, while $\frac{\partial c_j}{\partial r} > 0$ is analogous to the case where the income effect dominates. A similar logic applies to changes in the risk premium $\pi$. 
Note that, as an initial benchmark, we assume that $c_j(\cdot, \cdot)$ does not depend directly on passive portfolio share $\overline{\alpha}_p$.

The market clearing conditions are given by
\begin{equation}
    \sum_{j\in\{p,a\}} \omega_j C_j = Y, \qquad 
    \sum_{j\in\{p,a\}} \omega_j Q_j = 1, \qquad 
    \sum_{j\in\{p,a\}} \omega_j B_j = 0,\nonumber
\end{equation}
and the initial endowment of the risky asset satisfies $\sum_{j\in\{p,a\}} \omega_j Q_{j,-1} = 1$.

\paragraph{Market for risky assets.} Let $p = \log \frac{P}{Y}$ denote the log dividend-price ratio and $\mu \equiv \log \frac{\mathbb{E}[Y']}{Y}$ the log dividend growth. From the definition of the risk premium, we obtain the pricing condition
\begin{equation}\label{eq: risk_premium}
    \pi = \mu - p - r.
\end{equation}
Thus, given $p$ and $r$, we can solve for the risk premium. 

The demand for the risky asset for investor $j$ is given by $Q_j = \alpha_j [1 - c_{j}(r,\pi)]\frac{W_j}{P}$. Using Equation \eqref{eq: risk_premium}, we can express the demand for the risky asset as a function of $p$, $r$, and $\overline{\alpha}_p$: $Q_j = F_j(p, r, \overline{\alpha}_p)$.  
We can write the market clearing condition for the risky asset as follows:
\begin{equation}\label{eq: risky_mkt}
\underbrace{\omega_a F_a(p, r, \overline{\alpha}_p)}_{\text{active demand}}  = \underbrace{1 - \omega_p  F_p(p, r, \overline{\alpha}_p)}_{\text{net supply}}.
\end{equation}

The market for the risky asset reaches equilibrium when demand from active investors equals the net supply available to them---that is, the total supply minus the amount held by passive investors.
Notably, active demand is independent of $\overline{\alpha}_p$, meaning that changes in $\overline{\alpha}_p$ lead to shifts of the net supply curve. 
% In contrast, because $\alpha_p$ is not a function of the risk premium, the net supply is independent of the risk-free rate $r$, so changes in the interest rate lead to shifts in the demand curve of active investors. 

\paragraph{Market for goods.} 
The sensitivity of the consumption-wealth ratio to changes in the interest rate and the risk premium plays an important role in the analysis. 
% Standard portfolio theory imposes restrictions on the behavior of the  consumption-wealth ratio. 
In our dynamic model in Section~\ref{sec:model}, the average consumption-wealth ratio depends on $r+\pi$, meaning that sensitivity to interest rates is equal to sensitivity to the risk premium. 
Assumption~\ref{assump: c_w_property} below ensures that the consumption-wealth ratio in our simple model remains consistent with standard micro-founded models.
\begin{assump}\label{assump: c_w_property}
    The average consumption-wealth ratio is a function of the expected return in the risky asset, $r + \pi$, that is, $\sum_{j\in\{p,a\}} x_j c_j(r,\pi) = c(r+\pi)$, where $x_j \equiv \frac{\omega_j W_j}{\omega_a W_a + \omega_p W_p}$ is the wealth share. 
\end{assump}
Intuitively, since bonds are in zero net supply, the average return on investors’ portfolios corresponds to the return on the risky asset. The market-clearing condition for goods is given by:
\begin{equation}\label{eq: goods_mkt}
    c(\mu-p) = \frac{1}{1+e^p}.
\end{equation}
% The equilibrium values of $p$ and $r$ are determined by conditions \eqref{eq: risky_mkt} and \eqref{eq: goods_mkt}. 
Assumption~\ref{assump: c_w_property} implies that the system determining $p$ and $r$ satisfies an important \textit{recursivity property}: condition~\eqref{eq: goods_mkt} depends only on $p$, while condition~\eqref{eq: risky_mkt} depends on both $p$ and $r$. 

% The market-clearing condition for the goods market can be written as follows:
% \begin{equation}\label{eq:consumption_mkt_clearing1}
%    \underbrace{ \sum_{j\in\{p,a\}} x_j c_j(r, \mu-p-r)}_{\textbf{consumption-wealth ratio}} = \underbrace{\frac{1}{1+e^p}}_{\textbf{dividend yield}},
% \end{equation}
% where $x_j \equiv \frac{\omega_j W_j}{\omega_p W_a + \omega_p W_p}$ is the wealth share of investor $j$, and we used the fact that $\pi = \mu - p-r$ and $\sum_{j}\omega_j W_j = Y + P$. 
% The left-hand side of the expression above corresponds to the average consumption-wealth ratio, while the right-hand side corresponds to the ratio of dividends and the (cum-dividend) price, or the dividend yield. 

% The sensitivity of the average consumption-wealth to changes in the interest rate and the risk premium plays an important role in the analysis. 
% Standard portfolio theory imposes restrictions on the behavior of the  consumption-wealth ratio. 
% We show, in the context of our dynamic model in Section \ref{sec:model}, that the average consumption-wealth ratio depends on $r+\pi$, that is, the sensitivity to interest rates equals the sensitivity to the risk premium. 
% Assumption \ref{assump: c_w_property} below ensures that the consumption-wealth ratio in our simple model is consistent in this regard with standard micro-founded models.
% \begin{assump}\label{assump: c_w_property}
%     The average consumption-wealth ratio is a function of the expected return in the risky asset $r + \pi$, that is, there exits a function $c(\cdot)$ such that 
%    \begin{equation}
%         \sum_{j\in\{a,p\}} \omega_j c_j(r, \pi) = c(r+\pi).
%    \end{equation}
% \end{assump}
% Intuitively, as bonds are in zero net supply, the average return in investors' portfolio corresponds to the return on the risky asset. As the consumption-savings decision depends on the expected return on the portfolio, we obtain that the consumption-wealth ratio is a function of $r+\pi$. This implies that we can write the market-clearing condition for goods as follows:
% \begin{equation}\label{eq: goods_mkt}
%     c(\mu - p) = \frac{1}{1+e^p}.
% \end{equation}
% The equilibrium values of $p$ and $r$ are determined by conditions \eqref{eq: risky_mkt} and \eqref{eq: goods_mkt}. 
% Assumption \ref{assump: c_w_property} implies that the system satisfies an important \textit{recursivity property}: condition \eqref{eq: goods_mkt} depends only on $p$, while condition \eqref{eq: risky_mkt} depends on both $p$ and $r$. 
% To simplify the exposition, we further assume that $c_j(r, \pi) = c_j(r+\pi)$, but this is not essential for our results.

\subsection{The general equilibrium implications of portfolio flows}

We are interested in examining how the price of the risky asset, $p$, and the return on the riskless asset, $r$, respond to a portfolio flow toward the risky asset and away from the riskless bond. Let $\overline{\alpha}_p^\star$ denote the portfolio share of passive investors in an initial equilibrium, with corresponding prices  $(p^\star, r^\star)$. 
For a small deviation of the passive portfolio, $\overline{\alpha}_p = \overline{\alpha}_p^\star e^{\hat{\alpha}_p}$, we linearized the demand around the initial equilibrium to obtain $q_j \equiv \log Q_j / Q_j^\star$:
\begin{equation}
    q_j = - \zeta_{j,p}^q (p -p^\star) - \zeta_{j,r}^q (r -r^\star) + f_j,
\end{equation}
where $\zeta_{j,p}^q \equiv - \frac{\partial \log F_j}{\partial p}$ represents investor $j$'s price elasticity, and $\zeta_{j,r}^q \equiv - \frac{\partial \log F_j}{\partial r}$ denotes investor $j$'s \textit{cross-elasticity}, which measures the sensitivity of demand for the risky asset to changes in the price of the riskless asset.
The term $f_j \equiv \frac{\partial \log F_j}{\partial \log \overline{\alpha}_p} \hat{\alpha}_p$ represents a \textit{flow shock}, capturing an exogenous flow from bonds to stocks.
Since active demand is independent of $\overline{\alpha}_p$, we have $f_a = 0$, meaning only passive investors' demand is affected by the flow shock.
Moreover, because $\alpha_{p}$ is independent of the risk premium, the cross-elasticity for the passive investor is zero.


% We focus on the case where $Q_j^\star$, the number of shares held by investor $j$ in the initial equilibrium, coincides with their endowment at the beginning of the period, $Q_j^\star = Q_{j,-}$.
% Consider the equilibrium when $\overline{\alpha}_p = \overline{\alpha}_p^\star e^{\hat{\alpha}_p}$, where $\hat{\alpha}_p$ is small. 
% As $Q_j^\star = Q_{j,-}$, the case $\hat{\alpha}_p = 0$ corresponds to no trade in the risky asset in equilibrium. 
% If $\hat{\alpha}_p > 0$, then there is an inflow of resources in the market for the risky asset coming from the market for the riskless asset, while $\hat{\alpha}_p < 0$ corresponds to outflows from the market for the risky asset.

% We log-linearize the solution around the initial equilibrium. 
%  Linearizing $Q_j = F_j(p, r, \overline{\alpha}_p)$, we obtain $q_j \equiv \log Q_j / Q_j^\star$:
% \begin{equation}
%     q_{j} = \zeta_{j,0}^q - \zeta_{j,p}^q p - \zeta_{j,r}^q r + f_j,
% \end{equation}
% where $\zeta_{j,p}^q \equiv - \frac{\partial \log F_j(p^\star, r^\star, \overline{\alpha}_p^\star)}{\partial p}$ represents investor $j$'s price elasticity, $\zeta_{j,r}^q \equiv - \frac{\partial \log F_j(p^\star, r^\star, \overline{\alpha}_p^\star)}{\partial r}$ denotes investor $j$'s cross-elasticity, that is, the  sensitivity of demand for the risky asset with respect to the price of riskless asset.
% The term $f_j \equiv \frac{\partial \log F_j(p^\star, r^\star, \overline{\alpha}_p^\star)}{\partial \log \overline{\alpha}_p^\star} \hat{\alpha}_p$ represents a \textit{flow shock}, that is, a shock capturing an exogenous flow from bonds to stocks.
% As the active demand is independent of $\overline{\alpha}_p$, we have that $f_a = 0$, so only the demand of passive investors is subject to a flow shock.
% Given that $\alpha_{p}$ is independent of the risk premium, the cross-elasticity for the passive investor is zero, i.e., $\zeta_{p,r}^q = 0$.

% The market clearing condition for the risky asset can be written as 
% \begin{equation}
%     x_a q_a = - x_p q_p \implies \zeta_p^q (p-p^\star) + \zeta_r^q (r-r^\star) = f,\nonumber
% \end{equation}
% where $\zeta_k^q \equiv \sum_{j\in\{a,p\}} x_j \zeta_{j,k}^q$ , for $k \in \{p, r\}$, $f \equiv x_p f_p$, and we used the fact that $x_j = \omega_j Q_j^\star$. 
The right panel in Figure~\ref{fig:mkt_equilibrium} illustrates the equilibrium in the market for the risky asset. 
The solid downward-sloping curve represents active demand. An increase in $p$, given $r$, reduces asset demand by active investors, as it corresponds to a decline in the risk premium.  
The net supply of risky assets is depicted as the solid upward-sloping curve.
The net supply curve will be upward-sloping provided that $c'_p(r^\star+p^\star)$ is negative or sufficiently small.\footnote{
% The net supply is $1 - \omega_p \overline{\alpha}_p[1-c_p(\mu-p)] (1+e^{-p}) Q_{p,-1}$.
% , which increases in $p$ if $c_p'(\cdot)$ is sufficiently small.
Notice that the net supply would be vertical if we assumed that $Q_p$ was exogenously given instead of $\alpha_p$. 
}

The market-clearing condition for the goods market can be written as
\begin{equation}
    \zeta_{p}^c (p-p^\star) = -\frac{e^{p^\star}}{(1+e^{p^\star})^2} (p - p^\star),\nonumber
\end{equation}
 where $\zeta_p^c \equiv -c'(\mu-p^\star)$. The left-panel of Figure \ref{fig:mkt_equilibrium} shows the equilibrium in the goods market. 
 % We focus on the case where the average consumption-wealth ratio is increasing in $p$ (i.e., $\zeta_p^c >0$), so investors save less when expected returns are low. The right-hand side of the expression above corresponds to the dividend yield and it is decreasing in $p$.

% These equilibrium conditions can be written in matrix form:
% \begin{equation}
%     \left[\begin{matrix}
%         \zeta_p^q & \zeta_r^q\\
%         \zeta_p^c +\frac{e^{p^\star}}{(1+e^{p^\star})^2} & 0 
%     \end{matrix}\right] \left[ 
%     \begin{matrix}
%         p-p^\star\\
%         r-r^\star
%     \end{matrix}
%     \right] = 
%      \left[ 
%     \begin{matrix}
%         f\\
%         0
%     \end{matrix}
%     \right].
% \end{equation}
% By solving the system above, we are able to characterize the aggregate market elasticity, $\epsilon_M$, which we define as the inverse of the derivative of $p$ with respect to $f$, evaluated at $\hat{\alpha}_p = 0$:
% \begin{equation}
%     \epsilon_M \equiv \left(\frac{\partial p}{\partial f}\right)^{-1}.
% \end{equation}

% % Caption
% \node[align=center,font=\normalsize] at (6,-1.2)
%   {Figure 2.\quad Equilibrium in the goods and risky asset markets};

% \end{tikzpicture}
\paragraph{The inelastic markets hypothesis.} First, consider a \textit{partial equilibrium} version of this model, where we set $r = r^\star$ and drop the market clearing condition for goods. Alternatively, we could take the limit as $x_p \rightarrow 1$, in which case the cross-elasticity equals zero, $\zeta_r^q = 0$.\footnote{For this limit to be well-defined, we require $\zeta_p^c +\frac{e^{p^\star}}{(1+e^{p^\star})^2} = 0$, making the interest indeterminate.} Under this setup, the price of the risky asset satisfies:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:mkt-elasticity-pe}
    p - p^\star = \underbrace{\frac{1}{\zeta_p^q}}_{\substack{\text{inverse market}\\ \text{elasticity}}} \times \underbrace{f}_{\text{flow shock}},
\end{equation}
where $\zeta_p^q \equiv \sum_{j\in\{a,p\}} x_j \zeta_{j,p}^q$ and  $f \equiv x_p f_p$. 

Equation~\eqref{eq:mkt-elasticity-pe} shows that the price-dividend ratio responds to portfolio flows, with the price impact given by the inverse of market elasticity. 
This elasticity depends on the average price elasticity across all investors. 
This result is consistent with the \textit{inelastic market hypothesis} of \citet{gabaix2020search}, who estimate the price impact of flows into the stock market and find a large price response, suggesting that investors are relatively inelastic.  

Figure~\ref{fig:mkt_equilibrium} illustrates the intuition behind this result. When passive investors reallocate funds from the risky asset to the riskless asset, the net supply curve effectively shifts to the right, as shown by the upward-sloping dashed line in the right panel. To incentivize active investors to hold more of the risky asset, its expected return must increase---meaning the price-dividend ratio must decline. If both passive and active investors are relatively inelastic, then even a small flow shock can lead to a substantial price drop.% Figure~\ref{fig:mkt_equilibrium} illustrates the intuition behind this result. 
% If passive investors move funds from the risky asset to the riskless asset, this effectively shifts to the right the net-supply curve, as shown by the upward-sloping dashed line in the right panel. 
% To induce active investors to hold more of the risky asset, the expected return on the risky asset must increase, that is, the price-dividend ratio must fall. 
% If both passive and active investors are relatively inelastic, then the price must fall by a large amount even in response to a relatively small flow shock.

% This analysis, however, abstracts from the role of the cross-elasticity. 
% As funds flows out of the market for the riskless asset, it is assumed there is no reaction in the interest rate. 
% We consider next the price impact when the interest rate and the price of the risky asset are simultaneously determined. 

\paragraph{The crucial role of the cross-elasticity.} The partial equilibrium analysis abstracts from the role of cross-elasticity. 
The proposition below shows that the aggregate market elasticity is fundamentally different in the general equilibrium model where $r$ and $p$ are allowed to adjust. 

\begin{prop}[Infinite market elasticity]
    Suppose $\zeta_r^q \equiv \sum_{j\in\{a,p\}} x_j \zeta_{j,r}^q \neq 0$. Then, a portfolio flow shock has no price impact, $p -p^\star = 0$. Moreover, the portfolio flow shock has opposite effects on the risk premium and the interest rate: $r - r^\star = \frac{1}{\zeta_r^q} f$ and $\pi - \pi^\star = - \frac{1}{\zeta_r^q} f$. 
\end{prop}
% In this case, we obtain
% \begin{equation}
%     p = p^\star, \qquad r = r^\star + \frac{1}{\zeta_r^q} f, \qquad \pi = \pi^\star - \frac{1}{\zeta_r^q} f, 
% \end{equation}
% \begin{equation}
%       \left[ 
%     \begin{matrix}
%         p-p^\star\\
%         r-r^\star
%     \end{matrix}
%     \right] = \frac{1}{\zeta_r^q(\zeta_p^c + \frac{e^{p^\star}}{(1+e^{p^\star})^2})} \left[\begin{matrix}
%         0 & \zeta_r^q\\
%         \left(\zeta_p^c +\frac{e^{p^\star}}{(1+e^{p^\star})^2}\right) & -\zeta^q_p
%     \end{matrix}\right]
%      \left[ 
%     \begin{matrix}
%         f\\
%         0
%     \end{matrix}
%     \right],
% \end{equation}
% which implies that $p - p^\star =  0$ and $r - r^\star = \frac{1}{\zeta_r^q} f$. From Equation \eqref{eq: risk_premium}, we obtain $\pi - \pi^\star = - \frac{1}{\zeta_r^q} f$.
% Allowing for a non-zero cross-elasticity, $\zeta_{r}^q \neq 0$, has significant implications for the aggregate market elasticity. 
% Notably, the market is infinitely elastic, regardless of how price-inelastic each individual investors are, because  $p - p^\star$ is independent of $\zeta^q_p$. 
Give a non-zero cross-elasticity, $\zeta_{r}^q \neq 0$, the market is infinitely elastic, regardless of how price-inelastic individual investors are.
Even if investors are extremely price-inelastic ($\zeta_p^q \approx 0$), the market elasticity remains infinite. 
Moreover, the result holds for any non-zero value of the cross-elasticity. 
Markets would be infinitely elastic even with a small cross-elasticity $(\zeta^q_r \approx 0)$. 

The disconnect between individual price elasticity and market elasticity arises from a general equilibrium effect. Figure~\ref{fig:mkt_equilibrium} illustrates this mechanism.

In the goods market, the price-dividend ratio is determined by consumption behavior. This is clearly the case in models with unit elasticity of intertemporal substitution (EIS), where the price-dividend ratio is pinned down by the investor’s discount rate. Our formulation generalizes this approach.
% \footnote{With a unit EIS, the consumption-wealth ratio is independent of  $p$, meaning the average consumption-wealth ratio would be represented by a vertical line in the left panel of Figure \ref{fig:mkt_equilibrium}.} 
To restore equilibrium in the market for the risky asset, the interest rate must adjust until the demand from active investors meets the net supply at the initial price. This adjustment is represented in the right panel by the dashed downward-sloping curve, which shifts upward until it intersects the new net supply curve at the original price. Since the interest rate fully offsets movements in the risk premium, the price-dividend ratio remains unaffected by the flow shock.

Intuitively, as funds shift towards riskless bonds, the increased supply of bonds pushes interest rates down. 
Similarly, the reduction in the demand for risky assets (or the increase in net supply) raises the risk premium. 
In this economy, these two effects exactly offset each other, regardless of how price-inelastic individual investors are. 
If the decline in $r$ were smaller than the increase in risk premium, it would create excess demand for goods or, equivalently, an excess supply of bonds. 
Thus, in this economy, achieving simultaneous equilibrium in the markets for both risky and riskless assets requires an infinite market elasticity.
% Thus,   In this economy, a simultaneous equilibrium in the markets for the risky and riskless asset require an infinite market elasticity.

% \paragraph{Finite aggregate elasticity in general equilibrium.} We have seen that, in our general-equilibrium economy, the aggregate market elasticity is infinite, so flow shocks have no impact on the price of the risky asset. However, it is possible to obtain a finite elasticity even in general equilibrium. Consider the following extension: let's assume that the average consumption-wealth ratio also depends directly on $\overline{\alpha}_p$: $c(r+\pi, \overline{\alpha}_p)$. 
% In this case, a portfolio flow shock shifts not only the net supply of risky assets, but also the average consumption-wealth ratio. 
% Figure \ref{fig:mkt_equilibrium_positive_elasticity} illustrates this case. 
% The figure shows the effect of a portfolio flow away from risky assets, so the net-supply curve is shifted to the right as before. 
% However, we now allow for the possibility of the average consumption-wealth ratio curve to shift to the right, which corresponds to assume that $\frac{\partial c}{\partial \overline{\alpha}_p} < 0$. 
% In this case, the price-dividend ratio goes down, as the interest rate decline by less than the increase in risk premium. 
% Therefore, as portfolio outflows generate a drop in the price of the risky asset, there is positive price impact and a finite aggregate market elasticity.

% Notice that the price of the risky asset is given by 
% \begin{equation}
%     p - p^\star = - \frac{\zeta^c_{\overline{\alpha}_p}}{\zeta_p^c + \frac{e^{p^\star}}{(1+e^ {p^\star})^2}} \hat{\alpha}_p,
% \end{equation}
% where $\zeta^c_{\overline{\alpha}_p} \equiv \frac{\partial \log c(\mu-p^\star, \overline{\alpha}_p^\star)}{\partial \log \overline{\alpha}_p}$. 
% Notice that the aggregate market elasticity is finite and positive provided $\zeta^c_{\overline{\alpha}_p} < 0$. Hence, it depends on how the average consumption-wealth ratio responds to portfolio flow shocks. 
% Moreover, the aggregate market elasticity is independent of investors' price-elasticity $\zeta_p^q$, so again a general-equilibrium effect leads to a disconnect between individual and aggregate elasticities.

% \begin{figure}
%     \centering
% \tikzset{every picture/.style={line width=0.75pt}} %set default line width to 0.75pt        

% \begin{tikzpicture}[x=0.75pt,y=0.75pt,yscale=-1,xscale=1]
% %uncomment if require: \path (0,235); %set diagram left start at 0, and has height of 235

% %Shape: Axis 2D [id:dp05601326687806596] 
% \draw [color={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ,draw opacity=1 ][line width=1.5]  (50,200) -- (289,200)(70,39) -- (70,222) (282,195) -- (289,200) -- (282,205) (65,46) -- (70,39) -- (75,46) (101,195) -- (101,205)(132,195) -- (132,205)(163,195) -- (163,205)(194,195) -- (194,205)(225,195) -- (225,205)(256,195) -- (256,205)(65,169) -- (75,169)(65,138) -- (75,138)(65,107) -- (75,107)(65,76) -- (75,76) ;

% \draw (564,164) node  [color={rgb, 255:red, 65; green, 117; blue, 5 }  ,opacity=1 ,rotate=-25.74]  {$active\ demand$};
% % Text Node

% \draw (435,3) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]   [align=left] {\textbf{Market for risky asset}};
